Session 1

Remote Sensing, Visualization, and Spatial Data Applications for the Great Lakes

Chaired by George Leshkevich and Robert Shuchman

Contributions are invited on all aspects of remote sensing (including data collection, data analysis/interpretation, data applications, etc.) in the Great Lakes or other large lakes from satellite, airborne, ship, or other platforms. Presentations highlighting the role of remote sensing in interdisciplinary studies are encouraged, as are presentations describing the utilization of new or innovative sensors (such as scatterometer, hyperspectral, or acoustic) or techniques (such as data fusion and data visualization) for research or operational use.

Session 22

Plastics Research in the Great Lakes: Identifying Gaps and Facilitating Collaboration

Chaired by Sarah Zack, Melissa Duhaime and Carolyn Foley

The ecosystem impact of plastic litter (from nano- to micro- to macro-sized) in the Great Lakes is a growing area of research. While study of the fate, transport, and effects of plastic contamination in the oceans have been building for some time, similar knowledge in freshwater systems is greatly lacking. The impacts of this emerging contaminant will be difficult to manage in the future without a robust, well-coordinated, evidence-driven knowledge base. With that goal in mind, we welcome talks touching on all aspects of freshwater plastic research, including but not limited to the distribution and transport of plastic litter through the Great Lakes (or other freshwater) watershed, the potential for integration of plastics into Great Lakes food webs, modeling of ecosystem risks due to plastic pollution, description of the distribution and behavior of plastic-adsorbed organic pollutants, and technological innovation to prevent entry of plastic litter into freshwater systems. After scientific presentations have concluded, we will facilitate group discussion on the state of plastics research in the Laurentian Great Lakes. The goal of this discussion is to promote collaboration and exchange of information among academic, agency and NGO researchers and managers, and informally identify research priorities and data needs related to research of Great Lakes plastic litter.

Session 41

Modeling fish responses to changing habitat

Chaired by Alexander Jensen, Lisa Peterson and Alexander Maguffee

The Great Lakes and their associated tributaries face a host of ongoing changes, including accelerating barrier removals, warming waters, and eutrophication, among others. In coupling our understanding of fish population dynamics and environmental constraints, both empirical and conceptual models can provide the ability to better understand and predict how key populations may respond to these changing conditions. We therefore welcome any presentations on modeling fish responses to future changes in Great Lakes habitat quality or quantity.

Session 51

Using Bioindicators to Monitor Ecological Responses and Restoration Success

Chaired by David Mifsud and Maegan Stapleton

Several groups of fish and wildlife are recognized as biological indicators of water quality and ecosystem health. Assessing their presence on the landscape and at project sites can be an effective tool for documenting current site conditions and comparison to post modification, restoration, or enhancement. Presentations in this session will focus on the importance and utility of incorporating bioindicator monitoring into project plans and include case studies where this has been useful for measuring ecological responses and overall restoration success.

Session 43

Keeping it flowing: science and research in connecting channels of the Great Lakes

Chaired by Dimitry Gorsky, Knut Mehler, Edward F. Roseman and Lyubov E. Burlakova

Natural or constructed connecting channels are essential parts of the Great Lakes ecosystem. Flowing through rural and large urban areas connecting channels are not only tremendously important to the region's economy but they also house rich flora and fauna. Because of the high quantity and quality of these habitats, connecting channels were major sources of recruitment and production for many important sport and commercial fisheries. Connecting channels also provided critical habitat for currently threatened and endangered species such as Lake Sturgeon and Northern Madtom, natural connectivity between spawning habitat and downstream nursery habitats, and migration corridors for anadromous species. However, anthropogenic impacts have put an increasing pressure on many species inhabiting those water ways and degraded aquatic habitat in the corridors through development on land (e.g., urbanization, industrialization and associated pollutants), barriers and flow regulation, and dredging and channel modification. Habitat degradation included the loss of spawning grounds and wetland nursery areas, exotic species invasions, and disruption of fluvial connectivity. Many aquatic habitats were destroyed, made inaccessible, and/or disconnected from natural function. Challenges to these systems, as well as to other Great Lake tributaries, include tradeoff between maintaining the water ways for anthropogenic use and protection of their flora and fauna. Relevant topics for session will include, but not limited to, physical, chemical and biological processes in connecting channels and tributaries, aquatic monitoring and research , invasive species, AOC's, management initiatives, habitat mapping and restoration activities.

Session 10

Towards Development of a Great Lakes Early Warning System

Chaired by Lucinda Johnson, Michael Twiss, Matthew Child and Lizhu Wang

Governments have devoted significant resources to correct or remediate damage to the Great Lakes ecosystem. Examples include newly introduced chemicals that cause unforeseen impacts, the arrival of invasive species that alter the aquatic food web, and unanticipated changes in nutrient biogeochemistry leading to acute eutrophication issues. The International Joint Commission Science Advisory Board seeks to assess existing initiatives and issues that address emerging environmental stressors and threats and environmental early warning systems that could potentially be applicable to the Great Lakes.

Session 39

Water quality modeling in transboundary lake and river systems

Chaired by Glenn Benoy and Dale Robertson

Many lake and river systems that straddle borders suffer from symptoms of eutrophication, such as algal blooms and hypoxia. Lake Erie and elsewhere in the Laurentian Great Lakes, Lake Winnipeg downstream from the Red River, Lake of the Woods, and Missisquoi Bay of Lake Champlain are some of the well-known transboundary systems that are subject to excessive loading of nutrients from tributaries that drain watersheds managed by multiple agencies or groups. Tools and techniques that facilitate a shared understanding of nutrient loads, nutrient sources, and factors affecting nutrient delivery to rivers and lakes are important for the development of nutrient abatement and water quality protection and restoration policies. This session focuses on the development and application of water quality models such as SWAT, HSPF, and SPARROW, among others, in transboundary lake and river systems. Presentations are invited that address aspects of stream network development, harmonization of datasets, model calibration, and policy implications of model output. Presentations that cover geospatial processing and load estimation techniques that may contribute to water quality modeling, and applications involving coupled watershed-lake models are also invited.

Session 80

Data and Scientific Priorities for Managing Water Use in the Great Lakes-St. Lawrence River Basin

Chaired by Jon Allan, Mike Piskur and Jim Nicholas

In 2005, the Great Lakes States, Ontario and QuÃ©bec signed the Great Lakes-St. Lawrence River Basin Sustainable Water Resources Agreement. The Agreement details how the States and Provinces manage and protect water quantity in the Basin through an innovative structure of shared governance. This arrangement relies on coordinated actions undertaken by the individual jurisdictions within a regional framework. In recent years, the States and Provinces have made major improvements in the areas of water use information, cumulative impact analysis, and demand forecasting. At the same time, significant changes have occurred and are planned for regional water use including closure of major power generation facilities and expanded groundwater use in some areas. This session will highlight how regional progress on data collection and the integration of scientific priorities into decision making is resulting in better understanding and management of water use in the Basin.

Session 8

Disease and Pathogens of the Great Lakes and Freshwater Ecosystems.

Chaired by Kevin Strychar

Aquatic invasive species and their negative impact on recreation and economics is well recognized costing the US coastal and inland water billions of dollars annually and the Great Lakes Region approximately $200 million dollars per year. Less known, however, are the diseases and pathogens that these invasive species brought in with them. This session will focus on those aspects covering bacteria, fungi, and viruses, the invasive species they may have come from, and the endemic species they are affecting.

Session 75

Lessons Learned and New Tools for Aquatic Habitat Restoration

Chaired by Lynn Vaccaro, Tim Calappi, Ed Roseman and Matt Herbert

Restoring aquatic habitats for fish populations and ecosystem services is a complex process that requires the integration of diverse expertise and an adaptive management process. This session will highlight restoration projects designed to enhance and re-create aquatic habitat, including the development of fish spawning reefs and efforts to restore coastal wetlands in the Great Lakes. Presenters should be candid about challenges and unanticipated restoration results to help others learn from these experiences and find some common principles that can improve future habitat projects. Lessons learned and new tools could relate to any aspect of the restoration process, including project planning and design, the use numerical modeling to guide decisions or the integration of physical and biological assessment techniques.

Session 55

Discoveries, trends, and implications of chemicals in the Great Lakes

Chaired by David Pitts, Shawn McElmurry, Amina Salamova, Jiehong Guo, Elizabeth Murphy and Todd Nettesheim

Despite the significant progress achieved over the past few decades, toxic contaminants continue to persist in the Great Lakes ecosystem and remain a threat to human health and the environment. New chemical surveillance and discovery of has become a priority due to the potential of new chemicals to cause environmental problems. Knowledge of trends, occurrence, sources, transport, and fate of legacy contaminants, emerging contaminants, and endocrine disruptors are fundamental to the sound management of contaminants in the Great Lakes Basin. This session will highlight efforts to understand the use, presence and impacts of contaminants of mutual concern (as identified in the Annex 3 of the Great Lakes Water Quality Agreement (GLWQA)) and other contaminants through presentation of monitoring and surveillance data in the Great Lakes ecosystem along with biological effects measurements, in fish and wildlife populations. Discussions will focus on how these efforts support resource management decision-making and how the science supports the GLWQA, the Great Lakes Restoration Initiative, and the Canadian Chemicals Management Plan.

Session 18

Insights into mechanisms of ecological change from cross-lake comparisons

Chaired by Lars Rudstam, James Watkins, Lyubov Burlakova and Todd Nettesheim

Comparative analysis across systems can be a powerful approach for investigating hypotheses about mechanisms resulting in ecological change. With small lakes, it is often possible to use experimental approaches that include manipulations of whole lakes while observing similar lakes as control systems or to use large number of lakes to swamp the inherent variability among individual lakes. This is not possible in large lake systems which are rare and cannot be experimentally manipulated. The Laurentian Great Lakes (and other larger lakes in the area) do have similar geographical location and in several cases similar species composition, although each also have their own specific history of ecological change. But these differences among lakes can be exploited to investigate the likelihood of proposed mechanisms causing ecological change, especially if an anthropogenic or natural disturbance occur at different times and affecting part of the systems with data before and after a disturbance. An example would be the effects of invasives species that often become abundant at different times in different systems. Other changes may occur at the same time across lakes but be modified by specific attributes of each lake that can be used to gain understanding of mechanisms. In this session, we invite contributions that use a comparative approach across large lake ecosystems to gain insights into mechanisms that cause ecological change. Comparative data may be from within one research program sampling several lakes (such as the GLNPO program) or obtained by collaborations across research programs operating in individual lakes.

Session 22

Plastics Research in the Great Lakes: Identifying Gaps and Facilitating Collaboration

Chaired by Sarah Zack, Melissa Duhaime and Carolyn Foley

The ecosystem impact of plastic litter (from nano- to micro- to macro-sized) in the Great Lakes is a growing area of research. While study of the fate, transport, and effects of plastic contamination in the oceans have been building for some time, similar knowledge in freshwater systems is greatly lacking. The impacts of this emerging contaminant will be difficult to manage in the future without a robust, well-coordinated, evidence-driven knowledge base. With that goal in mind, we welcome talks touching on all aspects of freshwater plastic research, including but not limited to the distribution and transport of plastic litter through the Great Lakes (or other freshwater) watershed, the potential for integration of plastics into Great Lakes food webs, modeling of ecosystem risks due to plastic pollution, description of the distribution and behavior of plastic-adsorbed organic pollutants, and technological innovation to prevent entry of plastic litter into freshwater systems. After scientific presentations have concluded, we will facilitate group discussion on the state of plastics research in the Laurentian Great Lakes. The goal of this discussion is to promote collaboration and exchange of information among academic, agency and NGO researchers and managers, and informally identify research priorities and data needs related to research of Great Lakes plastic litter.

Session 51

Using Bioindicators to Monitor Ecological Responses and Restoration Success

Chaired by David Mifsud and Maegan Stapleton

Several groups of fish and wildlife are recognized as biological indicators of water quality and ecosystem health. Assessing their presence on the landscape and at project sites can be an effective tool for documenting current site conditions and comparison to post modification, restoration, or enhancement. Presentations in this session will focus on the importance and utility of incorporating bioindicator monitoring into project plans and include case studies where this has been useful for measuring ecological responses and overall restoration success.

Session 43

Keeping it flowing: science and research in connecting channels of the Great Lakes

Chaired by Dimitry Gorsky, Knut Mehler, Edward F. Roseman and Lyubov E. Burlakova

Natural or constructed connecting channels are essential parts of the Great Lakes ecosystem. Flowing through rural and large urban areas connecting channels are not only tremendously important to the region's economy but they also house rich flora and fauna. Because of the high quantity and quality of these habitats, connecting channels were major sources of recruitment and production for many important sport and commercial fisheries. Connecting channels also provided critical habitat for currently threatened and endangered species such as Lake Sturgeon and Northern Madtom, natural connectivity between spawning habitat and downstream nursery habitats, and migration corridors for anadromous species. However, anthropogenic impacts have put an increasing pressure on many species inhabiting those water ways and degraded aquatic habitat in the corridors through development on land (e.g., urbanization, industrialization and associated pollutants), barriers and flow regulation, and dredging and channel modification. Habitat degradation included the loss of spawning grounds and wetland nursery areas, exotic species invasions, and disruption of fluvial connectivity. Many aquatic habitats were destroyed, made inaccessible, and/or disconnected from natural function. Challenges to these systems, as well as to other Great Lake tributaries, include tradeoff between maintaining the water ways for anthropogenic use and protection of their flora and fauna. Relevant topics for session will include, but not limited to, physical, chemical and biological processes in connecting channels and tributaries, aquatic monitoring and research , invasive species, AOC's, management initiatives, habitat mapping and restoration activities.

Session 17

Great Lakes acoustic telemetry - from ecology to the restoration and management of fishes

Chaired by Scott Colborne, Darryl Hondorp and Steven Kessel

Freshwater fishes around the world are key components of healthy aquatic ecosystems in addition to the numerous species that are part of recreational and commercial fisheries supporting human populations and economies. The management of fish populations is facilitated through an understanding of spatial ecology, but the monitoring of fish habitat use and movement has long been a technical challenge for researchers and managers. Due to recent advancements in acoustic telemetry technology and the formation of international research collaborations, fishery managers increasingly are considering information about fish spatial ecology into the management decision-making process. This session will feature both the ecological characteristics of fish learned through acoustic telemetry and how this data informs restoration and management efforts of multiple fish species, providing insights into how telemetry could be employed across a variety of freshwater systems on a global scale.

Session 31

Connecting Researchers to CSMI through IAGLR

Chaired by Paris Collingsworth and Ed Verhamme

The Cooperative Science and Monitoring Initiative (CSMI) is a binational effort to improve cooperation between state, federal, academic and other scientists conducting research on the Great Lakes. The five year program has annual activities on every Great Lake culminating in an intense field year to answer key research questions. Traditionally the program has focused on government funded research, but an evolving partnership with IAGLR plans to assist State of the Lake planning committees with hosting rotating annual research reporting meetings. This session welcomes presentations and posters from the research community related to (CSMI) activities from across the Great Lakes including benefits to cooperating. The session will also include a panel discussion about CSMI goals and ways to encourage better connections to academic research. Finally the session will highlight the upcoming State of Lake Michigan conference to be held in November 2017 in Green Bay.

Session 75

Lessons Learned and New Tools for Aquatic Habitat Restoration

Chaired by Lynn Vaccaro, Tim Calappi, Ed Roseman and Matt Herbert

Restoring aquatic habitats for fish populations and ecosystem services is a complex process that requires the integration of diverse expertise and an adaptive management process. This session will highlight restoration projects designed to enhance and re-create aquatic habitat, including the development of fish spawning reefs and efforts to restore coastal wetlands in the Great Lakes. Presenters should be candid about challenges and unanticipated restoration results to help others learn from these experiences and find some common principles that can improve future habitat projects. Lessons learned and new tools could relate to any aspect of the restoration process, including project planning and design, the use numerical modeling to guide decisions or the integration of physical and biological assessment techniques.

Session 55

Discoveries, trends, and implications of chemicals in the Great Lakes

Chaired by David Pitts, Shawn McElmurry, Amina Salamova, Jiehong Guo, Elizabeth Murphy and Todd Nettesheim

Despite the significant progress achieved over the past few decades, toxic contaminants continue to persist in the Great Lakes ecosystem and remain a threat to human health and the environment. New chemical surveillance and discovery of has become a priority due to the potential of new chemicals to cause environmental problems. Knowledge of trends, occurrence, sources, transport, and fate of legacy contaminants, emerging contaminants, and endocrine disruptors are fundamental to the sound management of contaminants in the Great Lakes Basin. This session will highlight efforts to understand the use, presence and impacts of contaminants of mutual concern (as identified in the Annex 3 of the Great Lakes Water Quality Agreement (GLWQA)) and other contaminants through presentation of monitoring and surveillance data in the Great Lakes ecosystem along with biological effects measurements, in fish and wildlife populations. Discussions will focus on how these efforts support resource management decision-making and how the science supports the GLWQA, the Great Lakes Restoration Initiative, and the Canadian Chemicals Management Plan.

Session 18

Insights into mechanisms of ecological change from cross-lake comparisons

Chaired by Lars Rudstam, James Watkins, Lyubov Burlakova and Todd Nettesheim

Comparative analysis across systems can be a powerful approach for investigating hypotheses about mechanisms resulting in ecological change. With small lakes, it is often possible to use experimental approaches that include manipulations of whole lakes while observing similar lakes as control systems or to use large number of lakes to swamp the inherent variability among individual lakes. This is not possible in large lake systems which are rare and cannot be experimentally manipulated. The Laurentian Great Lakes (and other larger lakes in the area) do have similar geographical location and in several cases similar species composition, although each also have their own specific history of ecological change. But these differences among lakes can be exploited to investigate the likelihood of proposed mechanisms causing ecological change, especially if an anthropogenic or natural disturbance occur at different times and affecting part of the systems with data before and after a disturbance. An example would be the effects of invasives species that often become abundant at different times in different systems. Other changes may occur at the same time across lakes but be modified by specific attributes of each lake that can be used to gain understanding of mechanisms. In this session, we invite contributions that use a comparative approach across large lake ecosystems to gain insights into mechanisms that cause ecological change. Comparative data may be from within one research program sampling several lakes (such as the GLNPO program) or obtained by collaborations across research programs operating in individual lakes.

Session 58

Nutrient Sources, Transport & Retention Across Scales: Measurement, Modeling & Management

Chaired by Kimberly Van Meter, Chris Parsons, Nandita Basu and Philippe Van Cappellen

Non-point source nutrients are a major source of stress to Great Lakes watersheds, leading to the occurrence of harmful algal blooms, the development of offshore anoxic zones, and nuisance levels of Cladophora. Although significant efforts have been made in recent decades to implement agricultural best management practices and thus reduce nutrient loading to the lakes, eutrophication continues to be a significant problem, with the incidence of cyanobacterial blooms actually increasing since the mid-1990s. In this session, we welcome contributions related to nutrient (carbon, nitrogen, phosphorus, silica and sulfur) dynamics in Great Lakes watersheds, including inputs, retention mechanisms, and transport processes. We are particularly interested in work related to coupled nutrient cycles across a range of scales, from the whole watershed down to small, reactive interfaces.

Session 65

Fitting Dynamic Models to Time-Series Data

Chaired by Scott Peacor, John Marino, Samuel Truesdell and James Bence

Populations, communities and ecosystems of large lakes continue to experience substantial changes due to a range of natural and anthropogenic causes. Modern approaches to assessing these changes involve fitting dynamic models to time-series of data, so as to extract as much information as possible. Examples of such modeling include fishery stock assessments (fit to multiple time series simultaneously), tagging models (for tagging data), occupancy models (for presence-absence data), and state space models fit to survey indices of abundance (e.g., zooplankton collected over multiple years). We invite submissions of studies developing and/or applying approaches to make inferences about dynamics through examination of time-series data. Such time series could include habitat measures, characteristics of individual organisms (e.g., size-at-age), or measures of abundance (for a single taxon or for multiple interacting taxa). Our session will allow researchers in a range of disciplines to share ideas and challenges.

Session 13

Invasive Dreissenid Mussels: Ecology, Impacts, and Management

Chaired by Ashley Baldridge Elgin, Henry Vanderploeg and Donna Kashian

Benthic species play critical functional roles in aquatic ecosystems. Invasive benthic organisms thus have the capacity to affect lakes via altered nutrient cycling, diversion of energy flow through the food web, and changes in habitat and community composition. The Laurentian and other Great Lakes are host to many invasive benthic species from a broad range of taxonomic groups, including round goby, dreissenid mussels, rusty crayfish, and nuisance algae. Presentations in this session will cover multiple aspects of invasive benthic species, including ecology, impacts, and management efforts. This session also welcomes studies that make connections between benthic and pelagic processes.

Session 62

Protecting & Restoring Urban Watersheds: Using Green Infrastructure to Reduce Urban Runoff

Chaired by Jackie Adams, Danielle Green and Ralph Haefner

Stormwater runoff is a major cause of water pollution and flooding in urban areas. When rain falls on our roofs, streets, and parking lots in cities and their suburbs, the water cannot soak into the ground as it should. Instead, most developed areas rely on storm drains to carry large amounts of runoff, which carries pollutants such as sediment and chemicals, including road salt, lawn fertilizers, organic compounds, and metals, away from roofs and paved areas to nearby waterways. But "away" really means elsewhere, and that often results in flooding, increased flashiness, erosion and ultimately carrying greater amounts of pollutants into the Great Lakes. To protect surface-water quality and groundwater resources, development should be designed and built to minimize increases in runoff while addressing issues related to climate variability and potentially harmful alterations to the water resources of an urban area. Green infrastructure uses vegetation, soils, and other elements and practices to restore some of the natural processes required to manage water and create healthier urban environments. In this session, we invite contributions that will share challenges and successes of implementing green infrastructure projects in the Great Lakes watershed.

Session 45

How Do We Get There from Here? Application of models to inform water quality management

Chaired by Jennifer Read, Jay Martin and Scott Sowa

This session solicits presentations from teams working to apply models to guide water quality management. Models of interest range from probabilistic to mechanistic and can include human dimensions. The goal for the session is to develop a "modeling for water quality management" best practice list. Each presenter will be asked to focus on how the model has informed or guided management action and to close their presentation with their three top take-aways to undertaking this kind of applied research.

Session 72

A tribute to Jim Diana and his influence on Great Lakes research and management

Chaired by David Clapp, Solomon David and Joe Nohner

From the cold plains of Alberta to the hallowed halls of academia, in his 40+ years of fisheries work Jim Diana has had a significant impact on our understanding and management of Great Lakes systems. Despite all of his awards and accolades, his most important influence has been in serving as a mentor nonpareil to more than 70 graduate students at the University of Michigan. A majority of these students have worked on important Great Lake issues - from cutting edge management tools (regulation models, commercial fisheries management), to key species (lake sturgeon, yellow perch, alewife, muskellunge), to critical ecosystems (Saginaw Bay, Great Lakes tributaries). In this session, Jim's students and colleagues will pay tribute to an influential researcher, administrator, mentor, and friend by reviewing his career and the influence he's had in the Great Lakes and beyond.

Session 25

Harmful Algal Blooms (HABs) from watershed influence to ecosystem effects

Chaired by Mary Anne Evans, Timothy Davis, George Bullerjahn and Ken Gibbons

Harmful Algal Blooms (HABs) in the Great Lakes degrade water quality, deter recreation, and increase costs for water treatment plants. While causes of HABs are well known (i.e. nutrient loading, light, temperature), understanding exact sources of nutrients, triggers of toxicity, effective strategies to reduce nutrient loading, and effects on the lake ecosystem requires discipline spanning research that encompass agriculture, edge of field, tributaries, limnology, toxicology, food web ecology, human health, and other fields. Organized by the Great Lakes' HABs Collaboratory, this session will bring together research on HABs causes and consequences across the Great Lakes' basin.

Session 3

Restoring Great Lakes Areas of Concern

Chaired by John Hartig and Mohi Munawar

The goal of this symposium is to review and evaluate what has been achieved and learned through 32 years of Remedial Action Plans to restore Great Lakes Areas of Concern. Symposium talks will be by invitation only and all papers will be compiled into a books series published by the Ecovision World Monograph Series, Aquatic Ecosystem Health and Management Society, and Michigan State University Press.

Session 58

Nutrient Sources, Transport & Retention Across Scales: Measurement, Modeling & Management

Chaired by Kimberly Van Meter, Chris Parsons, Nandita Basu and Philippe Van Cappellen

Non-point source nutrients are a major source of stress to Great Lakes watersheds, leading to the occurrence of harmful algal blooms, the development of offshore anoxic zones, and nuisance levels of Cladophora. Although significant efforts have been made in recent decades to implement agricultural best management practices and thus reduce nutrient loading to the lakes, eutrophication continues to be a significant problem, with the incidence of cyanobacterial blooms actually increasing since the mid-1990s. In this session, we welcome contributions related to nutrient (carbon, nitrogen, phosphorus, silica and sulfur) dynamics in Great Lakes watersheds, including inputs, retention mechanisms, and transport processes. We are particularly interested in work related to coupled nutrient cycles across a range of scales, from the whole watershed down to small, reactive interfaces.

Session 65

Fitting Dynamic Models to Time-Series Data

Chaired by Scott Peacor, John Marino, Samuel Truesdell and James Bence

Populations, communities and ecosystems of large lakes continue to experience substantial changes due to a range of natural and anthropogenic causes. Modern approaches to assessing these changes involve fitting dynamic models to time-series of data, so as to extract as much information as possible. Examples of such modeling include fishery stock assessments (fit to multiple time series simultaneously), tagging models (for tagging data), occupancy models (for presence-absence data), and state space models fit to survey indices of abundance (e.g., zooplankton collected over multiple years). We invite submissions of studies developing and/or applying approaches to make inferences about dynamics through examination of time-series data. Such time series could include habitat measures, characteristics of individual organisms (e.g., size-at-age), or measures of abundance (for a single taxon or for multiple interacting taxa). Our session will allow researchers in a range of disciplines to share ideas and challenges.

Session 50

The Physical Systems of Large Lakes at Seasons to Millennia

Chaired by Brent Lofgren and Jia Wang

Variability and changes in climate are interactive among the air, land, water, and ice of the Great Lakes region, and link into the global atmosphere-ocean-surface system, as well as ecosystems regionally and globally. Hemispheric and global climatic phenomena can have different characteristics according to time scale--teleconnections on the seasonal time scale, greenhouse gases on the multi-decadal scale, and the genesis of the Laurentain Great Lakes in the aftermath of the last glacial maximum. We welcome contributions that address how lakes and their ecosystems interact with all aspects of the climate system at this wide range of time scales and in any lake-influenced region of the world.

Session 48

Big Lakes - Small World: Not all Great Lakes are Laurentian

Chaired by George Bullerjahn, Robert McKay, Boglarka Somogyi, John Lenters, Orlane Anneville, Lars Rudstam and Anne-Mari VentelÃ¤

Large lakes provide important ecosystem goods and services to our global society and are often characterized by pronounced physical, chemical, and trophic gradients. This is certainly true of the Laurentian Great Lakes, which are typically the focus of the IAGLR conference, but large lakes on other continents collectively span even broader gradients. These range from tropical lakes (e.g. Africa) to Arctic/subarctic lakes (e.g. Ladoga, Onego, Great Slave, Baikal), and temperate lakes (e.g. Balaton, Biwa, Geneva, Winnipeg). Despite the significant diversity among the world's great lakes, these important ecosystems are impacted by many common stressors such as climate change, eutrophication, invasive species, and land use changes. Alterations in lake function due to direct and indirect anthropogenic influences differ for each lake, yet often result in uncertain future threats to water quality and endemic species. Motivated by discussions at the 2015 European Large Lakes Symposium (ELLS), this session is devoted to gearing up for the first joint ELLS / IAGLR 2018 conference in France (Lake Geneva). Submissions are welcome from all aspects of global, large-lake limnology, particularly those that highlight the diversity and commonalities of the world's great lakes, their health and future trajectories, and presentations that stimulate ideas for future research priorities and special session topics for the 2018 joint conference.

Session 7

Improving model predictions through coupled system and data assimilation

Chaired by Philip Chu, Vincent Fortin, Pengfie Xue and Matthew Hoffman

Numerical models have proved to be a useful tool to understand, simulate and predict physical, biological and ecological processes. However, most models were developed and implemented in a standalone mode or loosely linked fashion to simulate a single process. With advances in high performance computing and software coupling, multiple models now have the capability to run in an integrated coupled mode, exchanging information dynamically. Model coupling has great potential to increase the accuracy of models in the Great Lakes, but it not widely used. With regards to data, the Great Lakes region has a fairly dense and long-term observational record of meteorological and physical parameters, especially compared to other coastal areas and deep oceans. These in-situ measurements and remotely sensed data have been widely used for model initialization and verification, but rarely get blended into model simulations to improve short-term forecasts or create reanalyses through data assimilation--which is one of the most effective approaches for statistically combining observational data and model dynamics to provide the best estimate of system state. This session invites contributions focusing on model improvement through model coupling (atmospheric, hydrologic, hydrodynamics, wave, ice, water quality and ecological models) and data assimilation.

Session 45

How Do We Get There from Here? Application of models to inform water quality management

Chaired by Jennifer Read, Jay Martin and Scott Sowa

This session solicits presentations from teams working to apply models to guide water quality management. Models of interest range from probabilistic to mechanistic and can include human dimensions. The goal for the session is to develop a "modeling for water quality management" best practice list. Each presenter will be asked to focus on how the model has informed or guided management action and to close their presentation with their three top take-aways to undertaking this kind of applied research.

Session 72

A tribute to Jim Diana and his influence on Great Lakes research and management

Chaired by David Clapp, Solomon David and Joe Nohner

From the cold plains of Alberta to the hallowed halls of academia, in his 40+ years of fisheries work Jim Diana has had a significant impact on our understanding and management of Great Lakes systems. Despite all of his awards and accolades, his most important influence has been in serving as a mentor nonpareil to more than 70 graduate students at the University of Michigan. A majority of these students have worked on important Great Lake issues - from cutting edge management tools (regulation models, commercial fisheries management), to key species (lake sturgeon, yellow perch, alewife, muskellunge), to critical ecosystems (Saginaw Bay, Great Lakes tributaries). In this session, Jim's students and colleagues will pay tribute to an influential researcher, administrator, mentor, and friend by reviewing his career and the influence he's had in the Great Lakes and beyond.

Session 25

Harmful Algal Blooms (HABs) from watershed influence to ecosystem effects

Chaired by Mary Anne Evans, Timothy Davis, George Bullerjahn and Ken Gibbons

Harmful Algal Blooms (HABs) in the Great Lakes degrade water quality, deter recreation, and increase costs for water treatment plants. While causes of HABs are well known (i.e. nutrient loading, light, temperature), understanding exact sources of nutrients, triggers of toxicity, effective strategies to reduce nutrient loading, and effects on the lake ecosystem requires discipline spanning research that encompass agriculture, edge of field, tributaries, limnology, toxicology, food web ecology, human health, and other fields. Organized by the Great Lakes' HABs Collaboratory, this session will bring together research on HABs causes and consequences across the Great Lakes' basin.

Session 58

Nutrient Sources, Transport & Retention Across Scales: Measurement, Modeling & Management

Chaired by Kimberly Van Meter, Chris Parsons, Nandita Basu and Philippe Van Cappellen

Non-point source nutrients are a major source of stress to Great Lakes watersheds, leading to the occurrence of harmful algal blooms, the development of offshore anoxic zones, and nuisance levels of Cladophora. Although significant efforts have been made in recent decades to implement agricultural best management practices and thus reduce nutrient loading to the lakes, eutrophication continues to be a significant problem, with the incidence of cyanobacterial blooms actually increasing since the mid-1990s. In this session, we welcome contributions related to nutrient (carbon, nitrogen, phosphorus, silica and sulfur) dynamics in Great Lakes watersheds, including inputs, retention mechanisms, and transport processes. We are particularly interested in work related to coupled nutrient cycles across a range of scales, from the whole watershed down to small, reactive interfaces.

Session 50

The Physical Systems of Large Lakes at Seasons to Millennia

Chaired by Brent Lofgren and Jia Wang

Variability and changes in climate are interactive among the air, land, water, and ice of the Great Lakes region, and link into the global atmosphere-ocean-surface system, as well as ecosystems regionally and globally. Hemispheric and global climatic phenomena can have different characteristics according to time scale--teleconnections on the seasonal time scale, greenhouse gases on the multi-decadal scale, and the genesis of the Laurentain Great Lakes in the aftermath of the last glacial maximum. We welcome contributions that address how lakes and their ecosystems interact with all aspects of the climate system at this wide range of time scales and in any lake-influenced region of the world.

Session 16

Application of trophic markers in aquatic ecology

Chaired by Austin Happel, Jacques Rinchard and Sergiusz Czesny

Trophic markers such as stable isotope ratios, fatty acid signatures, or algal pigments have become useful tools in studies of food web dynamics (e.g. trophic structure and trophic interactions) over the last two decades. As we learn more about the utility of these techniques, we gain a better understanding of trophic ecology both spatially and temporally. The objective of this session is to bring together scientists who use these markers to explore food webs in large lakes.

Session 48

Big Lakes - Small World: Not all Great Lakes are Laurentian

Chaired by George Bullerjahn, Robert McKay, Boglarka Somogyi, John Lenters, Orlane Anneville, Lars Rudstam and Anne-Mari VentelÃ¤

Large lakes provide important ecosystem goods and services to our global society and are often characterized by pronounced physical, chemical, and trophic gradients. This is certainly true of the Laurentian Great Lakes, which are typically the focus of the IAGLR conference, but large lakes on other continents collectively span even broader gradients. These range from tropical lakes (e.g. Africa) to Arctic/subarctic lakes (e.g. Ladoga, Onego, Great Slave, Baikal), and temperate lakes (e.g. Balaton, Biwa, Geneva, Winnipeg). Despite the significant diversity among the world's great lakes, these important ecosystems are impacted by many common stressors such as climate change, eutrophication, invasive species, and land use changes. Alterations in lake function due to direct and indirect anthropogenic influences differ for each lake, yet often result in uncertain future threats to water quality and endemic species. Motivated by discussions at the 2015 European Large Lakes Symposium (ELLS), this session is devoted to gearing up for the first joint ELLS / IAGLR 2018 conference in France (Lake Geneva). Submissions are welcome from all aspects of global, large-lake limnology, particularly those that highlight the diversity and commonalities of the world's great lakes, their health and future trajectories, and presentations that stimulate ideas for future research priorities and special session topics for the 2018 joint conference.

Session 7

Improving model predictions through coupled system and data assimilation

Chaired by Philip Chu, Vincent Fortin, Pengfie Xue and Matthew Hoffman

Numerical models have proved to be a useful tool to understand, simulate and predict physical, biological and ecological processes. However, most models were developed and implemented in a standalone mode or loosely linked fashion to simulate a single process. With advances in high performance computing and software coupling, multiple models now have the capability to run in an integrated coupled mode, exchanging information dynamically. Model coupling has great potential to increase the accuracy of models in the Great Lakes, but it not widely used. With regards to data, the Great Lakes region has a fairly dense and long-term observational record of meteorological and physical parameters, especially compared to other coastal areas and deep oceans. These in-situ measurements and remotely sensed data have been widely used for model initialization and verification, but rarely get blended into model simulations to improve short-term forecasts or create reanalyses through data assimilation--which is one of the most effective approaches for statistically combining observational data and model dynamics to provide the best estimate of system state. This session invites contributions focusing on model improvement through model coupling (atmospheric, hydrologic, hydrodynamics, wave, ice, water quality and ecological models) and data assimilation.

Session 47

What Good does it do Me? Engaging Community Stakeholders in the Implementation and Valuation of Green Infrastructure

Chaired by Paul Draus

This session focuses on the process of engaging community residents, businesses and nonprofit organizations in the implementation and valuation of green infrastructure projects. Though the environmental and economic benefits of green infrastructure (GI) may seem self-evident to some, communicating its social value to community residents and other stakeholders is essential to the long-term success of such projects. The projects described in this session offer a range of approaches to this problem: creating tools to communicate the value of GI, engaging local stakeholders in the process of GI implementation, and developing projects that serve multiple publics at once. By bringing together participants from multiple backgrounds and perspectives, this session will allow attendees to learn from each otherâ€™s experiences (successes and failures) and use these to inform their own present and future efforts.

Session 23

Bottom Mapping in the Laurentian Great Lakes: Physical, Biological and Cultural Features

Chaired by Brandon Krumwiede and Peter Esselman

Lake bottom environments are critical zones of interface between geology, the physical processes that control geomorphology, and biological processes that support healthy ecosystems and economies. Fine-scale information about bathymetry, bottom-type, biology, and archaeological resources are necessary to advance our understanding of lake properties and to support management decisions. Remote mapping technologies like sonar and LiDAR are integral to documenting spatial patterns in bottom features, and big data will be needed to understand the Laurentian Great Lakes at their full extent. This session will explore applications of remote technologies and big data to mapping the physical, biological, and cultural features of the Laurentian Great Lakes and their use to support decision making and management.

Session 57

Session 77

Advances in Understanding and Management of Non-native Species along the Invasion Curve

Chaired by Stephen Hensler, Lindsay Chadderton, Seth Herbst and John Navarro

Aquatic invasive species pose one of the most important problems threatening global aquatic ecosystems. This session highlights recent advances in our understanding of the pathways of introduction, development and application of surveillance methods and ongoing efforts to respond to recent invasions and to control the impacts of established invasive species in the Great Lakes.

Session 55

Discoveries, trends, and implications of chemicals in the Great Lakes

Chaired by David Pitts, Shawn McElmurry, Amina Salamova, Jiehong Guo, Elizabeth Murphy and Todd Nettesheim

Despite the significant progress achieved over the past few decades, toxic contaminants continue to persist in the Great Lakes ecosystem and remain a threat to human health and the environment. New chemical surveillance and discovery of has become a priority due to the potential of new chemicals to cause environmental problems. Knowledge of trends, occurrence, sources, transport, and fate of legacy contaminants, emerging contaminants, and endocrine disruptors are fundamental to the sound management of contaminants in the Great Lakes Basin. This session will highlight efforts to understand the use, presence and impacts of contaminants of mutual concern (as identified in the Annex 3 of the Great Lakes Water Quality Agreement (GLWQA)) and other contaminants through presentation of monitoring and surveillance data in the Great Lakes ecosystem along with biological effects measurements, in fish and wildlife populations. Discussions will focus on how these efforts support resource management decision-making and how the science supports the GLWQA, the Great Lakes Restoration Initiative, and the Canadian Chemicals Management Plan.

Session Chair Contact Information

David Pitts
Wayne State University
Pharmaceutical Sciences, 259 Mack Avenue

Session 40

Progress in Restoring Areas of Concern under the Great Lakes Restoration Initiative

Chaired by John Perrecone, Richard Hobrla and Matt Doss

This session will focus on the progress made since the 2010 Great Lakes Restoration Initiative in delisting AOCs and removing BUIs. It will focus on the process of AOC management and BUI removal, including partnership engagement, data collection and synthesis, overcoming challenges in the AOC delisting and BUI removal process, and the development of novel techniques to highlight remarkable progress EPA and its partners have made in cleaning up AOCs.

Session 66

Lessons from the frontiers in science for nutrient reduction from agriculture in the WLEB

Chaired by Michelle Selzer, Monica Day and Andrea Stay

Producers and partners of agriculture are working with scientists to find solutions to agricultural runoff to the Great Lakes. The session will highlight interdisciplinary, community-engaged research aimed at optimizing and accelerating implementation of conservation programs such as nutrient management, subsurface application, cover crops, modelling, peer-to-peer learning networks, transformative learning experiences, citizen science monitoring efforts, edge-of-field monitoring, social norms, and alternative economic incentive structures. The session will also highlight the transdisciplinary skills that can help researchers more effectively engage in rural communities. Each presentation will touch on both failures and successes. Attendees will also be invited to partner with session leaders on mini-projects to try out what they've learned back home in their watersheds.

Session 58

Nutrient Sources, Transport & Retention Across Scales: Measurement, Modeling & Management

Chaired by Kimberly Van Meter, Chris Parsons, Nandita Basu and Philippe Van Cappellen

Non-point source nutrients are a major source of stress to Great Lakes watersheds, leading to the occurrence of harmful algal blooms, the development of offshore anoxic zones, and nuisance levels of Cladophora. Although significant efforts have been made in recent decades to implement agricultural best management practices and thus reduce nutrient loading to the lakes, eutrophication continues to be a significant problem, with the incidence of cyanobacterial blooms actually increasing since the mid-1990s. In this session, we welcome contributions related to nutrient (carbon, nitrogen, phosphorus, silica and sulfur) dynamics in Great Lakes watersheds, including inputs, retention mechanisms, and transport processes. We are particularly interested in work related to coupled nutrient cycles across a range of scales, from the whole watershed down to small, reactive interfaces.

Session 16

Application of trophic markers in aquatic ecology

Chaired by Austin Happel, Jacques Rinchard and Sergiusz Czesny

Trophic markers such as stable isotope ratios, fatty acid signatures, or algal pigments have become useful tools in studies of food web dynamics (e.g. trophic structure and trophic interactions) over the last two decades. As we learn more about the utility of these techniques, we gain a better understanding of trophic ecology both spatially and temporally. The objective of this session is to bring together scientists who use these markers to explore food webs in large lakes.

Session 48

Big Lakes - Small World: Not all Great Lakes are Laurentian

Chaired by George Bullerjahn, Robert McKay, Boglarka Somogyi, John Lenters, Orlane Anneville, Lars Rudstam and Anne-Mari VentelÃ¤

Large lakes provide important ecosystem goods and services to our global society and are often characterized by pronounced physical, chemical, and trophic gradients. This is certainly true of the Laurentian Great Lakes, which are typically the focus of the IAGLR conference, but large lakes on other continents collectively span even broader gradients. These range from tropical lakes (e.g. Africa) to Arctic/subarctic lakes (e.g. Ladoga, Onego, Great Slave, Baikal), and temperate lakes (e.g. Balaton, Biwa, Geneva, Winnipeg). Despite the significant diversity among the world's great lakes, these important ecosystems are impacted by many common stressors such as climate change, eutrophication, invasive species, and land use changes. Alterations in lake function due to direct and indirect anthropogenic influences differ for each lake, yet often result in uncertain future threats to water quality and endemic species. Motivated by discussions at the 2015 European Large Lakes Symposium (ELLS), this session is devoted to gearing up for the first joint ELLS / IAGLR 2018 conference in France (Lake Geneva). Submissions are welcome from all aspects of global, large-lake limnology, particularly those that highlight the diversity and commonalities of the world's great lakes, their health and future trajectories, and presentations that stimulate ideas for future research priorities and special session topics for the 2018 joint conference.

Session 49

Binational and regional cooperation on invasive plant management - the case of Phragmites

Chaired by Dr. Pat Chow-Fraser, Dr. Kurt Kowalski and Heather Braun

Invasive Phragmites australis has resulted in catastrophic changes to terrestrial and wetland habitats across the Great Lakes basin, and continues to influence the character and coastal processes of shorelines. This session explores binational and regional approaches to manage and restore diverse site conditions including wetlands, coastal areas, and highway/road corridors across the Great Lakes basin, with a focus on moving toward a regional approach to eradicate this invasive plant.

Session 36

Regional Water Management: Development and Application of Modeling and Data for Decisions

Chaired by Lauren Fry, Andrew Gronewold, Jacob Bruxer and Deanna Apps

There is a long history of applying hydroclimate science for informing Great Lakes regulation and management that is not always well reflected in the peer reviewed literature. At the same time, recent advancements within the broader research community have the potential to improve management decisions in the region. This session aims to identify successful connections and potential gaps between water management operational practice and research developments in hydroclimate science, as well as potential approaches to resolving those gaps. We invite abstracts that document and evaluate existing applications of hydroclimate science for regulation and water management in the Great Lakes, investigate alternative methodologies in hydroclimate modeling for use in a regional water management setting, or propose new solutions for putting cutting edge research into practice within the Great Lakes water management community.

Lake Erie Harmful Algal Bloom Research Initiatives: Field to Faucet and Beyond

Chaired by Douglas Kane, Jay Martin and Christopher Winslow

In response to the Toledo Water Crisis of 2014, the Ohio Department of Higher Education (ODHE) and the College of Food, Agricultural, and Environmental Sciences (CFAES) at the Ohio State University initiated large research programs to deal with CHABs (Cyanobacterial Harmful Algal Blooms) in Lake Erie. The ODHE has funded Harmful Algal Bloom Research Initiatives to allow Ohio universities and their partners to Track Blooms from the Source, Protect Public Health, Produce Safe Drinking Water, and Engage Stakeholders. Meanwhile, both the CFAES "Field to Faucet" program and the Ohio Sea Grant Program have focused on "ensuring safe drinking water while keeping farms productive and profitable" and addressed how climate change, genomics, and nutrient availability and cycling influence CHABs. In this session, presenters from the above listed complimentary initiatives will detail the progress that has been made since the Toledo Water Crisis, with outside presentations dealing with Lake Erie CHABS also being welcome.

Session 57

Innovative Observations and Emerging Technologies

Chaired by Steven Ruberg, Kelli Paige, Thomas Johengen and Christopher Spence

Addressing many of the most pressing problems in the Great Lakes will require new, integrated data across spatial and temporal scales that have not been previously available. This session will explore development and deployment of innovative observing platforms, better integration of existing data sets, and improved access to the observations for ease of integration into models, operational forecasting and decision making.

Session 77

Advances in Understanding and Management of Non-native Species along the Invasion Curve

Chaired by Stephen Hensler, Lindsay Chadderton, Seth Herbst and John Navarro

Aquatic invasive species pose one of the most important problems threatening global aquatic ecosystems. This session highlights recent advances in our understanding of the pathways of introduction, development and application of surveillance methods and ongoing efforts to respond to recent invasions and to control the impacts of established invasive species in the Great Lakes.

Session 28

Watershed and Coastal Sediment Processes: Where is it going?

Chaired by Carol Miller, John Barkach, Jim Selegean and Rob Nairn

This session provides recent insights into both coastal erosion processes and sediment transport in the Great Lakes (from both an upland and shoreline perspective). Sediment movement from upland areas of the watershed to Great Lakes outlets can be a source of significant dredging demands, in terms of both costs and volumes. This session covers techniques that have been used to estimate sediment loading rates. The significance of various effects in impacting this loading rate are discussed, modeled, and quantified, including: land use, climatological functions, BMPs, and other. In addition, several presentations in this session provide information on physical coastal processes in the Great Lakes. These studies may include the quantification of waves and sediment transport along a shoreline or at a harbor or marina through either modeling or measurements.

Session 34

The Science and Policy of Multiple Stressors and Cumulative Effects in the Great Lakes

Chaired by Paul Sibley, Soren Brothers and Rene Shahmohamadloo

Research investigating the myriad stressors affecting the Great Lakes Basin has predominantly focused on individual stressor/stressor classes and this is reflected in most current policies regulating pollutants in the Great Lakes. Increasingly, however, it is recognized that multiple, individual stressors can interact in ways that are poorly understood, potentially leading to cumulative impacts that are not effectively addressed by policies based on an individual stressor approach. Understanding these interactions will be essential to predict future responses to ongoing (and new) interacting stresses and on which effective policies and management practices can be developed. In this session, we invite researchers to present their research on the interactions between environmental stressors in the Great Lakes and its watersheds, their potential cumulative effects, and the connections between the underlying science and current policies. By specifically addressing multiple stressors and cumulative effects, we hope to identify a broader foundation for future studies of the Great Lakes and its watersheds.

Session 68

Understanding drivers of benthic community condition in the Laurentian Great Lakes

Chaired by Elizabeth Hinchey, Glenn Warren, Lyubov Burlakova and Julie Lietz

Great Lakes benthic environments are dynamic habitats, subject to variable sources and rates of sediment delivery, abiotic forcing, and complex biotic processes. The role of these driving forces on benthic community structure and function must be understood when developing new indicators and approaches for benthic assessment. This session will bring together scientists studying benthic food webs, physicochemical disciplines (e.g., geology, biogeochemistry), and benthic assessment methods to identify and fill knowledge gaps concerning ecology of benthic communities in the Great Lakes systems. This session seeks submissions that address the roles of sediment chemistry, ecosystem engineering, advances in the characterization of sediment physical properties, or sediment disturbance on the structure, function, and assessment of nearshore and pelagic benthic environments. Novel approaches and exploratory analyses are encouraged.

Session 66

Lessons from the frontiers in science for nutrient reduction from agriculture in the WLEB

Chaired by Michelle Selzer, Monica Day and Andrea Stay

Producers and partners of agriculture are working with scientists to find solutions to agricultural runoff to the Great Lakes. The session will highlight interdisciplinary, community-engaged research aimed at optimizing and accelerating implementation of conservation programs such as nutrient management, subsurface application, cover crops, modelling, peer-to-peer learning networks, transformative learning experiences, citizen science monitoring efforts, edge-of-field monitoring, social norms, and alternative economic incentive structures. The session will also highlight the transdisciplinary skills that can help researchers more effectively engage in rural communities. Each presentation will touch on both failures and successes. Attendees will also be invited to partner with session leaders on mini-projects to try out what they've learned back home in their watersheds.

Session 2

The new age of ballast water management in the Great Lakes

Chaired by Jeffrey Ram

Numerous non-native organisms have entered the Great Lakes in ballast water discharged in Great Lakes ports. With the final ratification in September 2016 of the International Maritime Organization's Ballast Water Convention (IMO-BWC), the treaty will go into force in September 2017. This symposium considers how the IMO-BWC will affect the science, technology, management, enforcement, and economics of ballast water management in the Great Lakes.

Session 14

Thiamine deficiency in the Great Lakes - a recurring issue

Chaired by Jacques Rinchard, Matt Futia and Donald Tillitt

Since the late 1960's, thiamine deficiency has been shown to have dramatic organismal and ecosystem consequences in wildlife populations (e.g., fishes, birds, mammals). The degradation of thiamine by the enzyme thiaminase is one of the mechanisms by which thiamine deficiency can occur. Through this session, we will bring scientists together to update our knowledge of this recurring issue, discuss the latest monitoring data, present new analytical methods to assess the deficiency, and explore the causative agents of this thiamine deficiency (e.g., thiaminase, unbalanced diet rich in fat).

Session 49

Binational and regional cooperation on invasive plant management - the case of Phragmites

Chaired by Dr. Pat Chow-Fraser, Dr. Kurt Kowalski and Heather Braun

Invasive Phragmites australis has resulted in catastrophic changes to terrestrial and wetland habitats across the Great Lakes basin, and continues to influence the character and coastal processes of shorelines. This session explores binational and regional approaches to manage and restore diverse site conditions including wetlands, coastal areas, and highway/road corridors across the Great Lakes basin, with a focus on moving toward a regional approach to eradicate this invasive plant.

Session 36

Regional Water Management: Development and Application of Modeling and Data for Decisions

Chaired by Lauren Fry, Andrew Gronewold, Jacob Bruxer and Deanna Apps

There is a long history of applying hydroclimate science for informing Great Lakes regulation and management that is not always well reflected in the peer reviewed literature. At the same time, recent advancements within the broader research community have the potential to improve management decisions in the region. This session aims to identify successful connections and potential gaps between water management operational practice and research developments in hydroclimate science, as well as potential approaches to resolving those gaps. We invite abstracts that document and evaluate existing applications of hydroclimate science for regulation and water management in the Great Lakes, investigate alternative methodologies in hydroclimate modeling for use in a regional water management setting, or propose new solutions for putting cutting edge research into practice within the Great Lakes water management community.

Session 11

Lake Erie Harmful Algal Bloom Research Initiatives: Field to Faucet and Beyond

Chaired by Douglas Kane, Jay Martin and Christopher Winslow

In response to the Toledo Water Crisis of 2014, the Ohio Department of Higher Education (ODHE) and the College of Food, Agricultural, and Environmental Sciences (CFAES) at the Ohio State University initiated large research programs to deal with CHABs (Cyanobacterial Harmful Algal Blooms) in Lake Erie. The ODHE has funded Harmful Algal Bloom Research Initiatives to allow Ohio universities and their partners to Track Blooms from the Source, Protect Public Health, Produce Safe Drinking Water, and Engage Stakeholders. Meanwhile, both the CFAES "Field to Faucet" program and the Ohio Sea Grant Program have focused on "ensuring safe drinking water while keeping farms productive and profitable" and addressed how climate change, genomics, and nutrient availability and cycling influence CHABs. In this session, presenters from the above listed complimentary initiatives will detail the progress that has been made since the Toledo Water Crisis, with outside presentations dealing with Lake Erie CHABS also being welcome.

Session 30

Freshwater, Fresh Ideas: Great Lakes Research and Innovative Industries

Chaired by Ed Verhamme and Kathryn Buckner

From providing drinking water to cities to cooling water for power plants our industrial users of Great Lakes water rely daily on their knowledge of the lakes to serve us safely. Historically, universities and governments worked very closely with industry to transfer knowledge and assist with research needs, but in the last few decades there has been a shift towards isolation rather than cooperation. This session is meant to highlight the research efforts and needs of the private sector across the Great Lakes and encourage cooperation with other researchers. Areas where cooperation has benefited researchers and operators include monitoring HABs in Lake Erie and tracking debris that could clog power plant intakes in Lake Michigan. Presentations are welcomed from researchers, operators, and managers.

Session 64

Relevance of bacterial, archaeal, and viral dynamics to Great Lakes ecosystem processes

Chaired by Vincent Denef and Melissa Duhaime

This session will focus on the fundamental roles that bacteria, archaea, and their viruses play in freshwater community ecology and ecosystem functioning. Community dynamics are influenced by bacterial and archaeal biomass fluxes that support the higher food web via zooplankton grazing. Ecosystem functioning is affected as bacteria and archaea are key determinants of nutrient concentrations and fluxes by fixing carbon and nitrogen, assimilating and remineralizing dissolved and particulate organic matter, and oxidizing and reducing a range of other elements. These contributions are influenced by interactions with their viruses. Recent methodological advances now allow for more in-depth analyses of these highly diverse communities and their contributions to food web dynamics and geochemical cycling. Despite an increased attention to these communities in the Great Lakes, they are often not included in system models. We hope to bring together researchers studying the importance of bacteria, archaea, and their viruses to Great Lakes ecosystem processes using a variety of survey, experimental, and modeling approaches.
